Six:
[1] His statement: 'And concerning a house... etc.'; al-'Ayni said in 'al-Binaya': 'Know that the 'ushri lands are:
First: The land of the Arabs like Hijaz and Yemen.
Second: Land whose people embraced Islam willingly. Third: Land conquered by force and divided among the spoilers. Fourth: Land revived and irrigated with 'ushr water. Fifth: The kharaji land whose kharaj water ceased, so it was irrigated with 'ushri water. Sixth: He made his house a garden and irrigated it with 'ushr water.
And the kharaji lands are eight:
First: That which was conquered by force and left in their hands with kharaj levied upon it, as 'Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) did in the land of Sawad al-Iraq and Egypt. Second: Land revived by a dhimmi infidel with the Imam's permission, or [another case]... Third: He made his house a garden even if he irrigated it with 'ushr water. Fourth: Some infidels asked the Imam to impose kharaj on their lands without... Fifth: Land irrigated with kharaj water. Sixth: Land bought by a Muslim from an infidel. Seventh: The 'ushri land when its 'ushr water ceased and it was irrigated with kharaj water. Eighth: A Muslim [who had] a house and made it a garden and irrigated it with kharaj water. All this was mentioned by al-Walwaliji in his 'Fatawa'.'
[2] His statement: 'a garden'; it is land surrounded by a wall with scattered trees. So in 'Mi'raj al-Diraya'.

[PAGE_466]
'Umda al-Ri'aya 'ala Sharh al-Wiqaya
Kharaj if it belongs to a dhimmi, or to a Muslim who irrigated it with his water
[1]
'Kharaj if it belongs to a dhimmi, or to a Muslim who irrigated it with his water': i.e., with kharaj water. If he did not make it a garden and there were palm trees in it, nothing is due on them, and similarly nothing is due on the fruit of a house-garden. So in 'al-Bahr' and others.
11] His statement: 'Kharaj'; if it belongs to a dhimmi, meaning whether he irrigated it with 'ushr water or with kharaj water, because the infidel is subject to kharaj, not 'ushr.
Kharaj (with fatha) is that which the Imam imposes on the infidel, and it is of two types: 1. Kharaj muqasama: proportional kharaj - that which the Imam imposes on land conquered by force and upon which he is gracious to its people, from half, third, or quarter of the produce. 2. Kharaj wazifa: fixed kharaj - that which the Imam fixes upon it, as 'Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) did on the land of Sawad al-Iraq; and details of all that will come in its place from the 'Book of Jihad'. (2) His statement: 'irrigated it with his water'; know that whoever revives dead land with the Imam's permission from among that which the Muslims conquered, and the Imam divided it among the soldiers, then it is 'ushri land; because when he divided it among the Muslims it became 'ushri land.
If the Imam left it in the hands of its infidel inhabitants, then it is kharaji land.
If one revives dead land from the lands made waqf for Muslims and the like which are not 'ushri nor kharaji, then water is considered; if he irrigates it with kharaj water then it is kharaji, and if he irrigates it with 'ushr water then it is 'ushri. An objection was raised on the issue: that it necessitates the obligation of kharaj on a Muslim from the beginning, and they have stated that it is not obligatory on him from the beginning. And it was answered: that what is prohibited is imposing kharaj initially by compulsion; but by his choice it is permissible, and he has consented here by irrigating it with kharaj water. So mentioned by al-'Atabi. And al-Zayla'i and Ibn al-Humam mentioned that when a Muslim irrigates with kharaj water, the water transfers its obligation to the land, so there is no imposition of kharaj on the Muslim from the beginning, rather it is a transfer of what was its function...
Source pages p. 465

Commentary
This passage is a commentary by the author of 'Umda al-Ri'aya on the matn of al-Wiqaya, specifically on the section dealing with land taxation (kharaj and 'ushr). The author begins by quoting al-'Ayni from 'al-Binaya', who lists six categories of 'ushri lands (lands subject to the tithe of one-tenth on produce) and eight categories of kharaji lands (lands subject to kharaj tax). The 'ushri lands include: the original lands of the Arabs like Hijaz and Yemen; lands whose inhabitants accepted Islam voluntarily; lands conquered by force and distributed among the Muslim soldiers; lands revived and irrigated with water from an 'ushri source; kharaji land whose kharaj water source dried up and was replaced by 'ushri water; and a house turned into a garden irrigated with 'ushri water. The kharaji lands include: lands conquered and left in the hands of the original inhabitants with kharaj, as 'Umar did in Sawad al-Iraq; lands revived by a dhimmi with the Imam's permission; a house turned into a garden even if irrigated with 'ushri water (if owned by a dhimmi); lands on which infidels requested the Imam to impose kharaj; lands irrigated with kharaj water; lands bought by a Muslim from an infidel; 'ushri land that becomes irrigated with kharaj water; and a Muslim's house turned into a garden and irrigated with kharaj water. The commentator then clarifies that a 'bustan' (garden) is land enclosed by a wall with scattered trees.

Moving to page 466, the commentator explains the phrase 'kharaj if it belongs to a dhimmi, or to a Muslim who irrigated it with his water'. The 'his water' in the matn means kharaj water (i.e., water from a source subject to kharaj). If a person does not turn his house into a garden but has palm trees in it, no tax is due on the trees or their fruit. For a dhimmi, kharaj is due regardless of whether he uses 'ushri or kharaji water, because an infidel is liable for kharaj, not 'ushr. Kharaj is defined as the tax imposed by the Imam on the infidel, and it is of two types: proportional (muqasama), which is a share of the produce (half, third, quarter, etc.), and fixed (wazifa), a fixed annual amount. For a Muslim who revives a dead land, the ruling depends on the water source: if it was part of conquered land divided by the Imam among Muslims, it is 'ushri; if left to the original infidel inhabitants, it is kharaji. For dead land that is neither 'ushri nor kharaji (like waqf land), the water source determines the tax status: irrigation with kharaj water makes it kharaji; with 'ushr water makes it 'ushri. The commentator addresses an objection: normally, kharaj cannot be imposed on a Muslim from the start. Here, however, the Muslim voluntarily uses kharaj water, so he has consented to the obligation. Al-Zayla'i and Ibn al-Humam explained that the water transfers its own tax obligation to the land, so it is not an imposition of kharaj on the Muslim but a transfer of the water's function. The passage ends abruptly, likely continuing on the next page.

Key Terms
الأراضي العشرية (al-arādī al-ʿushriyya): Lands subject to the 'ushr tax (one-tenth of produce), including original Arab lands, lands of voluntary converts, conquered lands divided among Muslims, revived lands with 'ushri water, etc.
الأراضي الخراجية (al-arādī al-kharājiyya): Lands subject to kharaj tax, typically conquered lands left in the hands of non-Muslims or lands irrigated with kharaj water.
خراج مقاسمة (kharāj muqāsama): Proportional kharaj: a share (e.g., half, third, quarter) of the land's produce taken as tax.
خراج وظيفة (kharāj wazīfa): Fixed kharaj: a fixed annual amount of tax imposed on land regardless of produce.
بستان (bustān): A garden enclosed by a wall with scattered trees.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when does a Muslim become liable for kharaj on a house turned into a garden?
A) When the house is in a conquered land.
B) When the garden is irrigated with kharaj water.
C) When the garden contains fruit trees.
D) When the garden is enclosed by a wall.

Key Takeaway
A Muslim's garden becomes subject to kharaj only when irrigated with kharaj water, because the water transfers its tax obligation to the land.

Land Tax Classification by Water Source
Determining land tax status based on water and ownership

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for land being classified as 'ushri or kharaji and apply the ruling based on water source and owner's religion.

Core Idea
A Muslim's voluntary use of kharaji water makes the land kharaji, though kharaj cannot be initially imposed by force.

This lesson unpacks the Hanafi ruling on land tax classification. You might assume that only non-Muslim land is subject to kharaj, but the text shows that a Muslim who irrigates with kharaji water voluntarily accepts the kharaj liability. The key is the water source: 'ushri water (rain, wells, springs) keeps land 'ushri; kharaji water (rivers dug by non-Muslims) makes it kharaji. Understanding this distinction is essential for proper zakat and tax calculations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the landowner and water source
First, determine if the landowner is a Muslim or a dhimmi, and what water is used for irrigation (rain, well, spring, or river dug by non-Muslims).
2
Classify the water as 'ushri or kharaji
Rain, well, spring, and sea water are 'ushri. Rivers originally dug by non-Muslims (e.g., Tigris, Oxus) are kharaji. If the water source is unknown or likely Islamic, it is ruled 'ushri.
The presumption: water from wells/springs seen today is considered Islamic unless proven otherwise.
3
Apply the ruling for dhimmi land
If the owner is a dhimmi, the land is always kharaji regardless of water source, because a dhimmi is liable for kharaj, not 'ushr.
4
Apply the ruling for Muslim land
If the owner is a Muslim, the land follows the water: 'ushri water → 'ushri land; kharaji water → kharaji land. Mixed irrigation (both types) results in 'ushri land (the Muslim is more suitable for 'ushr).
This applies even if the Muslim revives dead land; the water source determines the status.
5
Resolve the objection on initial imposition
An objector asks: How can kharaj become wajib on a Muslim when it is normally not imposed on Muslims? The answer: The prohibition is on compulsory imposition; here the Muslim chooses to use kharaji water, thus voluntarily accepting the kharaj. Alternatively, the water transfers its function to the land (like buying kharaji land).
This shows the Hanafi concern for avoiding force while respecting choice.

Kharaj (with fatha) is what the Imam imposes on the disbeliever, and it is of two types: 1) Kharaj muqasama: what the Imam imposes on land conquered and granted to its people, from half, third, or quarter of the produce. 2) Kharaj wazifa: what the Imam fixes upon it, as 'Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) did on the land of Sawad of Iraq, and details will come in the Book of Jihad. (2) His saying: irrigates it with its water; know that whoever revives dead land with the Imam's permission from what the Muslims conquered and the Imam divided among the army, it is 'ushr land, because when he divided it among the Muslims it became 'ushri land. If one revives dead land from the lands endowed for Muslims and similar which are neither 'ushri nor kharaji, then water is considered: if he irrigates it with kharaj water it becomes kharaji, and if he irrigates it with 'ushr water it becomes 'ushri. An objection was raised: that it necessitates the obligation of kharaj on a Muslim initially, and they explicitly said it is not initially obligatory. The answer: the prohibition is the imposition of kharaj initially by force, but by his choice it is permissible, and he has accepted it here when he irrigated with kharaj water. Thus al-'Utabi mentioned it. Al-Zayla'i and Ibn al-Humam mentioned that if a Muslim irrigates with kharaji water, the water transfers with its function to the land, so there is no imposition of kharaj on the Muslim initially, but rather it is a transfer of what is its function, as if he bought kharaji land. And if he irrigates it with 'ushr water it is 'ushr. Rainwater, well water, spring water are 'ushri. Water of rivers dug by the non-Arabs is kharaji, and likewise Sayhun, Jayhun, and Dijla. [1] (And if he irrigates it with 'ushr water it is 'ushr. [2] Rainwater, well water, spring water are 'ushri, and water of rivers dug by non-Arabs is kharaji): like the river of Yazdajird and similar. (And likewise Sayhun, Jayhun, and Dijla) the kharaj goes to it with its function, as if he bought kharaji land. [1] His saying: with 'ushr water; and likewise if he irrigates with both, because the Muslim is more suitable for 'ushr. [2] His saying: 'ushri; the summary is that water over which the disbelievers had control, then Muslims took it by force, is kharaji; what is other than that is 'ushri. From that: rainwater descending from the sky via clouds, well water, spring water, and sea water which does not fall under anyone's authority. If you say: well and spring waters should be kharaji because they are considered booty, since we took control over them by force. I say: that does not apply to every well and spring, for most of what was dug by disbelievers has been obliterated, and what we see now is either known to have originated in Islam or of unknown status, so it is ruled as Islamic, attributing the event to its nearest time. Thus in Fath al-Qadir. [3] His saying: non-Arabs, i.e., disbeliever 'Ajam like Kisra and Yazdajird (with fatha on the underdotted ya, sukun on the zay, fatha on the dal, then kasra on the jim, then ra, then dal: the Arabized Yazdgard, the last king of Persia, killed in the caliphate of 'Uthman may Allah be pleased with him). So the small rivers in the lands of the 'Ajam which the disbelievers dug and the Muslims took control over by force and conquest are kharaji. [4] His saying: Sayhun (with fatha on the sin) is the name of the river of the Turks. Jayhun (on its pattern) is the name of the river of Balkh, and it is said the river of Tirmidh. Dijla (with kasra on the dal) is the river of Baghdad. Al-Furat (with damm) is the river of Kufa.
Source pages p. 466

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the chapter on al-Kharaj (land tax) within the Book of Zakat. The central ruling is that the tax classification of land—whether it is 'ushri (subject to tithe) or kharaji (subject to land tax)—depends on the water used for irrigation and the religious status of the owner. The text explains that if the land belongs to a dhimmi (non-Muslim subject), it is always kharaji regardless of the water source, because a dhimmi is liable for kharaj, not 'ushr. For a Muslim, the ruling is more nuanced: if the Muslim irrigates the land with 'ushri water (rain, wells, springs), the land is 'ushri; if he irrigates with kharaji water (from rivers dug by non-Muslims, like the Tigris, Oxus, etc.), the land becomes kharaji. The commentary addresses an objection: imposing kharaj on a Muslim initially is normally prohibited, but here it is permissible because the Muslim chose to use kharaji water, thus voluntarily accepting the liability. Alternatively, scholars like al-Zayla'i and Ibn al-Humam argue that the water transfers its legal function to the land, so it is not an initial imposition but a transfer analogous to purchasing kharaji land. The passage also defines kharaj as the tax imposed by the imam on non-Muslims, divided into muqasama (proportional) and wazifa (fixed), and provides examples of water sources and their classifications.

Key Terms
خراج (kharāj): A tax imposed by the imam on non-Muslims on agricultural land; it is of two types: fixed (wazifa) and proportional (muqasama).
عشر ('ushr): A tithe (10% or 5%) on agricultural produce from Muslim-owned land that is 'ushri.
ماء الخراج (mā' al-kharāj): Water from sources that were originally under non-Muslim control, such as rivers dug by Persians; using it makes the land kharaji.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) 'Ushri
B) Kharaji
C) No tax
D) It depends on whether the land was originally 'ushri

Key Takeaway
Land classification depends on the irrigation water source and owner's religion; a Muslim who voluntarily uses kharaji water accepts kharaj liability.

Tax Classification of Irrigation Water: The Four Rivers Dispute
Classifying irrigation water for 'ushr/kharaj and resolving the river dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Abu Yusuf classifies the four major rivers as kharaji, evaluate Muhammad's counter-argument, and identify the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence) of the dispute.

Core Idea
The presence of bridges (qanantir) on a river indicates Muslim possession, making it kharaji; otherwise, it is 'ushri like rainwater.

This passage from 'Umda al-Ri'aya presents a classical intra-Hanafi dispute about the tax classification of the four great rivers: Sayhun, Jayhun, Dajla (Tigris), and Furat (Euphrates). The ruling determines whether farmers using these rivers pay 'ushr (a lower tax) or kharaj (a higher land tax). The disagreement turns on a single factual question: do these rivers show signs of Muslim control (yad) or are they like unprotected seas? We will walk through each imam's proof and the principle behind the classification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue and the two positions
The dispute is about the four rivers: Abu Yusuf (and Abu Hanifa according to some reports) considers them kharaji; Muhammad al-Shaybani considers them 'ushri.
The base rule: water over which Muslims have physical possession (yad) after conquering the land is kharaji; otherwise it is 'ushri.
2
Examine Abu Yusuf's dalil
Abu Yusuf argues that the construction of bridges (qanantir) on these rivers is evidence of possession (yad) by Muslims, thus they fall under kharaj.
Bridges are man-made structures that demonstrate human control and authority over the water.
3
Examine Muhammad's dalil
Muhammad counters that these rivers are not protected or guarded by anyone; they flow freely, so they are analogous to seas and rainwater, which are 'ushri.
He focuses on the absence of active protection, not the historical presence of structures.
4
Understand the principle of default classification
The commentator also clarifies that for wells and springs, the default is 'ushri unless there is certain evidence of non-Muslim origin. If the origin is unknown, it is attributed to the Islamic era due to the principle of attaching to the nearest time.
This principle (al-hadath al-aqrab) is used to resolve uncertainty.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and tarjih
The practical consequence: if kharaji, farmers pay kharaj; if 'ushri, they pay 'ushr. The text does not give a final tarjih, but later practice often follows Abu Yusuf due to the widespread prevalence of irrigation structures.
The mufta bihi in many regions is Abu Yusuf's view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الزكاة / باب الخارج
وإن سقاها بماء العَشْرِ عُشِّر ، وماء السَّماء، والبئر ، والعين عشري، وماء أنهار حفرها الأعاجم خراجي ، وكذا سيحون، وجَيْحُون، ودجلة والفرات عند أبي يوسف ، وعشري عند محمد له.
( وإن سقاها بماء العَشْرِ عشر.
وماء السماء، والبئر ، والعين عشري ، وماء أنهار حفرَها الأعاجم " خراجي) : كنهر يَزْدَجِرد ونحوه ، ( وكذا سَيْحُون، وجَيْحُون، ودجلة والفرات عند أبي يوسف ، وعشري عند محمد له.

قوله : عشري ؛ جملة القول فيه أنّ الماء الذي كانت للكفرة يد عليه ، ثم حواه أهل الإسلام قهراً خراجي، وما سواه عشري، فمنه : ماء السماء النازل من السماء بواسطة السحاب، ومنه : ماء الآبار، ومنه ماء العيون، ومنه : ماء البحر الذي لا يدخل في ولاية أحد. فإن قلت : ينبغي أن تكون مياه الآبار والعيون خراجية ؛ لأنها في حكم الغنيمة، حيث حوينا عليها قهرا .

قوله : عند أبي يوسف الله ؛ لأنها تتخذ عليها القناطر من السفن، وهذا آية اليد عليها، فهي داخلة تحت الولاية، ومحمد الله يقول : إن هذه الأنهار لا يحميها أحد، فصارت كالبحار ومياه الأمطار.
The Book of Zakat / Chapter of Kharaj
And if he irrigates it with 'ushr water, then 'ushr is due; and (with) rainwater, well water, and spring water, it is 'ushri; and water of rivers dug by non-Arabs is kharaji, and likewise Sayhun, Jayhun, Dajla, and the Euphrates according to Abu Yusuf, and 'ushri according to Muhammad.
(And if he irrigates it with 'ushr water, then 'ushr; and rainwater, well water, and spring water is 'ushri; and water of rivers dug by non-Arabs is kharaji): like the river of Yazdijird and the like, (and likewise Sayhun, Jayhun, Dajla, and the Euphrates according to Abu Yusuf, and 'ushri according to Muhammad.
His saying: 'with 'ushr water' – and likewise if he irrigates with both (types); because the Muslim is more suited to 'ushr.
His saying: 'ushri' – the summary statement about it is that water which the disbelievers had a hand over, then the people of Islam overpowered it by force, is kharaji; and other than that is 'ushri. Among these are: rainwater descending from the sky via clouds; well water; spring water; and sea water which is not under anyone's authority. If you say: well water and spring water should be kharaji because they are in the category of booty, since we overpowered them by force. I say: This does not necessarily apply to every well and spring, for most of what was dug by disbelievers has been obliterated, and what we see now is either known to have originated in Islam, or its status is unknown; so it is judged to be Islamic, attributing the event to the nearest of its two times. As in Fath al-Qadir.
His saying: 'non-Arabs' – meaning the disbelievers of the non-Arabs, like Kisra and Yazdijird, with a fatha on the ya, sukun on the zay, fatha on the dal, then a kasra on the jim, then ra and dal – a Persian name (Yazdgird), the last king of Persia, killed during the caliphate of Uthman, may Allah be pleased with him. So the small rivers in the lands of the non-Arabs that were dug by disbelievers, and the people of Islam overpowered them by force and compulsion, are kharaji.
His saying: 'Sayhun' – with a fatha on the sin, name of the river of the Turks. Jayhun – on the same measure, name of the river of Balkh, and it is said the river of Tirmidh. Dajla – with a kasra on the dal, the river of Baghdad. And the Euphrates – with a damma, the river of Kufa.
And the Euphrates according to Abu Yusuf, and 'ushri according to Muhammad.
His saying: 'according to Abu Yusuf' – because bridges of ships are constructed upon them, and this is an indication of possession over them, so they fall under authority; and Muhammad says: these rivers are not protected by anyone, so they become like seas and rainwater.
Source pages p. 467

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the tax classification of the four major rivers (Sayhun, Jayhun, Dajla, and Furat). The base text from Al-Wiqayah states that water from rivers dug by non-Arabs (like Yazdijird) is kharaji. The author then explains that the same ruling applies to the four rivers according to Abu Yusuf, but according to Muhammad they are 'ushri. The commentator (author of 'Umda al-Ri'aya) elaborates: Abu Yusuf argues that because bridges (qanatir) are built on these rivers, this demonstrates control (yad) by the Muslims, making them subject to kharaj like other conquered land. Muhammad counters that these rivers are not guarded or protected by anyone, so they are like seas and rainwater, thus 'ushri. The commentary also clarifies that other waters (rain, wells, springs) are 'ushri unless there is certain evidence of non-Muslim origin, in which case the default is to attribute them to Muslim times due to the principle of attaching the event to the nearest time.

Key Terms
عشري ('ushri): Land or water that is subject to 'ushr (1/10 or 1/20 tax on produce) because it is conquered peacefully or owned by Muslims.
خراجي (kharaji): Land or water that is subject to kharaj (land tax) because it was conquered by force and remains in the hands of the original owners or is state land.
قناطر (qanatir): Bridges or aqueducts built on rivers; their presence indicates human control and possession (yad).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the ruling on rivers dug by non-Arabs (e.g., the river of Yazdijird)?
A) They are 'ushri
B) They are kharaji
C) They are subject to a fixed tax
D) They are exempt from tax

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the classification of a water source as 'ushri or kharaji hinges on whether Muslims have physical possession (yad) over it, as evidenced by structures like bridges.

Tax Obligations on Mineral Springs in 'Ushr and Kharaj Lands
Clarifying tax obligations on mineral springs in 'ushr and kharaj lands

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on tax ('ushr/kharaj) for pitch and naphtha springs and distinguish between the tax liability on the spring itself and its arable surrounding land (harim).

Core Idea
Mineral springs are not agricultural produce; 'ushr is on produce, kharaj is on cultivable land, not the spring itself.

This passage from Al-Wiqayah clarifies a subtle point in Hanafi land tax law. While 'ushr and kharaj are typically associated with agricultural land, what happens when a spring of pitch or naphtha emerges? The ruling distinguishes between the spring itself and the surrounding land (harim) that can be cultivated. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying land tax rules in the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two land types
Note that the passage discusses two categories of land: 'ushr land (land owned by Muslims, subject to 'ushr on produce) and kharaj land (land conquered by treaty, subject to kharaj on the land).
'Ushr land is typically agricultural land that pays 'ushr (a 10% or 5% tax on yield). Kharaj is a fixed land tax.
2
Distinguish between the spring and its harim
The ruling treats the spring itself as separate from the surrounding land (harim). The harim is the arable area that can be cultivated. Tax is assessed on the harim if cultivable, not on the spring.
The harim is defined as the land suitable for cultivation around the spring.
3
Apply the ruling for 'ushr land
In 'ushr land, no 'ushr is due on the spring itself, because the spring is not produce (ghallah) of the land; it is like a water spring. Only actual agricultural produce is subject to 'ushr.
The passage states: 'ولا شيء في عين قير ونفط في أرض عشر' – nothing on the spring.
4
Apply the ruling for kharaj land
In kharaj land, kharaj is due on the arable harim if it is suitable for cultivation, but not on the spring itself. Kharaj is a tax on the land's productive capacity, not on the spring.
The passage says: 'وفي أرض خراج في حريمها الصالح للزراعة خراج لا فيها' – kharaj on the harim, not on the spring.
5
Understand the rationale
The commentator explains that the spring is not from the growth (nama') of the land; it is a gushing source like water. 'Ushr is linked to produce, kharaj to land's potential for cultivation. The spring itself is not taxed.
Refer to the commentary from Al-Binayah cited in the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا شيء في عين قير ونفط في أرض عشر، وفي أرضِ خَراج في حريمها الصالح للزراعة خراج لا فيها : أي إن كان حريمُ العين صالحاً للزراعة يجب فيه الخراج لا في العينين.
قوله : في عين قير؛ هو بالكسر الزفت، ويقال : القار أيضاً. والنفط؛ بفتح النون وكسرها وهو الأصح، دهن يعلو الماء في العين، والوجه في عدم وجوب العشر منه أنه ليس من ربع الأرض ونمائها، وإنما هو عين فوارة، كعين الماء. كذا في «البناية»
قوله : يجب فيه؛ أي الحريم؛ لأن وجوب الخراج بالتمكن من الزراعة، وأما العشر فيجب في حريمها العشري إن زرعه وإلا لا؛ لتعلقه بالخارج.
And nothing (is due) on a spring of pitch (qir) and naphtha in 'ushr land; and in kharaj land, on its harim (arable surrounding) suitable for cultivation, kharaj is due, not on the spring itself. That is, if the harim of the spring is suitable for cultivation, kharaj is obligatory on it, not on the spring(s).
His saying: 'on a spring of qir' – it is, with kasra, pitch, and also called qar. And naphtha – with fatha on the nun and kasra, the latter being more correct – is oil that rises on water in the spring. The rationale for the non-obligation of 'ushr on it is that it is not from the produce and growth of the land, but rather it is a gushing spring, like a water spring. As in Al-Binayah.
His saying: 'it is obligatory on it' – meaning the harim; because the obligation of kharaj depends on the capability of cultivation; as for 'ushr, it is obligatory on its 'ushri harim if he cultivates it, otherwise not, because it is linked to the produce.
Source pages p. 468

Commentary
This passage states a ruling on mineral springs (pitch and naphtha) in relation to land tax. The base text from Al-Wiqayah says: in 'ushr land, no 'ushr is due on the spring itself; in kharaj land, kharaj is due only on the arable land surrounding (harim) the spring, not on the spring itself. The commentator explains: pitch (qir) and naphtha (naft) are like water springs – they are not produce of the land (ghallah) but rather a natural resource that gushes forth. Therefore, they are not subject to 'ushr because 'ushr is linked to agricultural produce. For kharaj land, the tax is on the potential for cultivation; thus only the harim (the land around the spring that can be farmed) is taxed, not the spring itself. This ruling is consistent with the Hanafi principle that kharaj is a tax on the land's capacity for cultivation, while 'ushr is a tax on actual produce.

Key Terms
حريم (harim): The surrounding land of a spring that is suitable for cultivation.
قير (qir): Pitch (bitumen), a thick black substance from springs.
نفط (nafṭ): Naphtha, a type of oil that rises on water in springs.
أرض عشر (ard 'ushr): Land owned by Muslims on which 'ushr (a 10% or 5% tax) is paid on agricultural produce.
أرض خراج (ard kharaj): Land conquered by treaty that is subject to a fixed land tax (kharaj).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In kharaj land, kharaj is due on the _____ of a pitch spring.
A) spring itself
B) harim (arable surrounding land)
C) pitch extracted
D) both spring and harim

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, mineral springs like pitch and naphtha are not subject to 'ushr or kharaj themselves; only the arable surrounding land (harim) may be taxed if it is cultivable.

Rulings on Euphrates, Bitumen, and Oil Springs
Intra-Hanafi dispute on river taxation and ruling on non-agricultural springs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on the Euphrates river and explain why no 'ushr is due on bitumen/oil springs, citing the proofs (talil).

Core Idea
Bitumen and oil are not land produce, so no 'ushr; the Euphrates is under state authority due to human structures.

This passage deals with two distinct but related issues: the classification of the Euphrates river and the taxation of bitumen and oil springs. Abu Yusuf and Abu Hanifa hold that the Euphrates falls under state authority because of human constructions, while Imam Muhammad considers it like public water. For bitumen and oil springs, the school agrees that no 'ushr is due because these are not agricultural products; kharaj applies only to the surrounding cultivable land.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two legal issues
The passage presents two rulings: (1) the Euphrates river's classification (state authority vs. 'ushri), and (2) taxation on bitumen and oil springs.
Separate the river dispute from the spring ruling.
2
Analyze the Euphrates dispute
Abu Yusuf (with Abu Hanifa) argues the Euphrates is under state authority because ships' arches (qanatir) are built on it, indicating human possession. Muhammad argues it is like seas and rainwater, not reserved, hence 'ushri.
The footnote confirms Abu Hanifa agrees with Abu Yusuf.
3
Examine the ruling on bitumen and oil
In 'ushr land, no 'ushr is due on bitumen or oil springs. The reason (talil) is that these are not from the earth's produce or growth but gushing springs like water.
In kharaj land, kharaj is due only on the harim if cultivable, not on the spring itself.
4
Understand the ta'lil for both
For the Euphrates, the proof is physical structures (qanatir) indicating control. For bitumen, the proof is that it is not agricultural produce—it is a natural substance not dependent on cultivation.
These proofs are based on 'urf (custom) and analogy to water springs.

The Euphrates: according to Abu Yusuf it is [under state authority], and according to Muhammad it is 'ushri. Nothing is due in a bitumen or oil spring in 'ushr land, and in kharaj land, its harim (environs) if suitable for cultivation, then kharaj is due on it, not in the spring. [1] He said: according to Abu Yusuf, because ships' arches are built on it, this indicates possession, so it falls under authority. Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the rulings on the Euphrates river and on bitumen and oil springs. The matn states that the Euphrates is considered a state-managed waterway according to Abu Yusuf, thus subject to the ruler's authority, while Imam Muhammad considers it like an 'ushri water source. The commentator explains that Abu Yusuf's view stems from the fact that ships' arches (qanatir) are built on it, indicating human control, whereas Muhammad likens it to reservoirs and rainwater which are not privately owned. The footnote clarifies that Abu Hanifa agrees with Abu Yusuf. Regarding bitumen (qir) and oil (naft) springs, the ruling is that no 'ushr is due on them in 'ushr land because they are not agricultural produce; they are natural springs like water. In kharaj land, only the harim (surrounding land) is subject to kharaj if it is cultivable, not the spring itself, because kharaj is based on the potential for cultivation, not the produce itself.

Key Terms
عين قير ('ayn qir): A spring of bitumen (pitch), a thick black substance used for waterproofing.
نفط (naft (or nift)): Oil or petroleum that floats on the surface of a spring.
حريم (harim): The surrounding land or reserve area of a spring that is suitable for cultivation.
عشر ('ushr): Land tax of 10% on agricultural produce of 'ushr lands (Muslim-owned).
خراج (kharaj): Land tax on kharaj lands (conquered lands remaining with non-Muslims), payable annually.

Key Takeaway
The Euphrates is ruled under state authority per Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf due to human structures, and bitumen/oil springs are exempt from 'ushr because they are not agricultural produce.

Categories of Zakat Recipients and the Status of Mu'allaf
Explanation of the eight zakat categories and the dropping of mu'allaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the eight categories of zakat recipients from Quran 9:60 and explain why the Hanafi school considers the mu'allaf category to be defunct, distinguishing between naskh and cessation due to end of cause.

Core Idea
The mu'allaf category ended not by abrogation, but because its cause expired when Islam became strong.

This passage opens the chapter on the disbursement categories (masarif) of zakat. It first identifies the eight categories mandated by Quran 9:60, then explains that one category — those whose hearts are reconciled (mu'allafatu qulubuhum) — has lapsed due to the consensus of the Companions after the death of the Prophet. The commentator carefully distinguishes this cessation from naskh (abrogation), clarifying a key usul principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the eight categories from the verse
Read Quran 9:60 as cited in the text and list all eight categories: fuqara, masakin, amilun, mu'allaf, riqab, gharimun, fi sabilillah, ibn al-sabil.
The word 'إنما' indicates restriction (hasr).
2
Understand the historical context of mu'allaf
The Prophet gave to certain individuals to reconcile hearts, strengthen weak faith, or avoid harm. After Islam became strong, the need ceased.
3
Analyze the ijma of the Companions
After the Prophet's death, Umar rejected the claim of mu'allaf, and Abu Bakr confirmed it in the presence of the Companions, creating a binding consensus.
Ijma is a source of law capable of ending a ruling based on a specific cause.
4
Distinguish between naskh and cessation of cause
The commentator explains that this is not naskh (abrogation by revelation) but rather intiha' al-shay' bi-intiha' sababihi—the end of the ruling due to the end of its cause.
Naskh only occurs via revelation; cessation of cause is a different mechanism.
5
Apply the ruling to current Hanafi practice
The mufta bihi (operative ruling) is that the mu'allaf category is not an active recipient of zakat today.
This is the unanimous Hanafi position.

Source Text

باب المصارف) [١] قوله : باب المصارف ؛ أي مصارف الزكاة والعشر، وهي مصارف أيضاً لصدقة الفطر والكفارة والنذر ، وغير ذلك من الصدقات الواجبة، والأصل فيه قوله تعالى : إِنَّمَا الصَّدَقَاتُ لِلْفُقَرَاءِ وَالْمَسَكِينِ وَالْعَامِلِينَ عَلَيْهَا وَالْمُؤَلَّفَةِ قُلُوبُهُمْ وَفِي الرِّقَابِ وَالْغَرِمِينَ وَفِي سَبِيلِ اللَّهِ وَابْنِ السَّبِيلِ ) فهذه ثمانية أصناف، وقد سقط منها صنف واحد ، وهو المؤلفة قلوبهم، فقد كان رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم يعطيهم ليسلموا أو يسلم قومهم لإسلامهم، ومنهم من كان أسلم وفيه ضعف، فيعطيه ليتقرر الإسلام في قلبه ، ومنهم من كان يعطيه خوفاً من شرهم وأذاهم. فلما قبض رسول الله جاؤوا إلى أبي بكر الله لأخذ سهمهم، وإلى عمر ه ، : إن رسول الله له ما كان يعطيكم ليؤلفكم على الإسلام، فأما اليوم فقد أعز الله دينه ، فليس بيننا وبينكم إلا السيف أو الإسلام، فانصرفوا إلى أبي بكر له وقالوا : أنت الخليفة أم عمر ، فأقر أبو بكر الله رأي . ه ، وأبطل سهمهم، وكان بمحضر من الصحابة لله ، فصار إجماعاً منهم على سقوطه. كذا في «البناية»(۳) ، فقال . عمر ذلك وغيره. عمر فإن قلت : كيف ينسخ إجماعُ الصحابة الله وآراء الرجال السهم المذكور في القرآن والسنة. قلت : لا نسخ هاهنا ، بل هو من قبيل انتهاء الشيء بانتهاء سببه، وتحقيقه في كتب الأصول (٤).
Chapter of the disbursement categories: i.e., the categories for zakat and 'ushr, and they are also the categories for sadaqat al-fitr, kaffarah, nadhr, and other obligatory charities. The basis is the verse: 'Indeed the charities are for the poor, the needy, the workers over them, and those whose hearts are reconciled, and for freeing slaves, and the debtors, and in the way of Allah, and the wayfarer.' These are eight categories. One category has lapsed, namely those whose hearts are reconciled. The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) used to give them so that they would become Muslim or their people would become Muslim through their conversion, and among them were those who had converted but were weak, so he would give to strengthen Islam in their hearts, and some were given out of fear of their harm. When the Messenger of Allah died, they came to Abu Bakr (May Allah be pleased with him) to take their share, and to Umar (May Allah be pleased with him) who said: 'The Messenger of Allah used to give you to reconcile your hearts to Islam; today Allah has strengthened His religion. There is nothing between us and you but the sword or Islam.' So they went back to Abu Bakr and said: 'Are you the caliph or Umar?' Abu Bakr affirmed Umar's opinion and cancelled their share, and this was in the presence of the Companions, so it became a consensus on its cancellation. So in al-Binayah. Then Umar said that and so on. If you say: How can the consensus of the Companions and opinions of men abrogate a share mentioned in the Quran and Sunnah? I say: There is no abrogation here; rather it is a cessation of the thing due to the cessation of its cause, and its verification is in the books of usul.
Source pages p. 469

Commentary
This passage opens the chapter on the disbursement categories (masarif) of zakat. It first identifies that the same eight categories apply to zakat, 'ushr, sadaqat al-fitr, kaffarah, and other mandatory charities. The primary textual basis is Quran 9:60. The commentator then explains that one category — mu'allafatu qulubuhum (those whose hearts are to be reconciled) — has lapsed. He provides historical context: the Prophet would give to certain individuals to encourage conversion, to strengthen weak faith, or to avoid harm. After the Prophet's death, representatives of this category approached the caliphs. Umar rejected their claim, stating that Islam is now strong, and offered only the sword or Islam. Abu Bakr confirmed Umar's judgment in the presence of the Companions, establishing a consensus (ijma') on the cancellation. The commentator then addresses a potential objection: how can consensus abrogate a Quranic ruling? He clarifies that this is not naskh (abrogation) but rather the end of the category due to the end of its cause (intiha' al-shay' bi-intiha' sababihi). The ruling was conditional upon the need for reconciliation, and once the need ceased, the category naturally expired.

Key Terms
مصارف (masarif): The categories or channels for disbursement of zakat and other obligatory charities.
المؤلفة قلوبهم (al-mu'allafatu qulubuhum): Those whose hearts are to be reconciled; new converts or potential allies given zakat to strengthen their faith or secure their goodwill.
إجماع (ijma'): Consensus of the Companions on cancelling the share for mu'allaf.
نسخ (naskh): Abrogation of a Quranic ruling by another revelation. The commentator distinguishes it from the cessation of a ruling due to the end of its cause.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is the mu'allaf category no longer applicable?
A) Because the verse was abrogated by another verse.
B) Because the Prophet explicitly cancelled it before his death.
C) Because the cause for its existence disappeared after Islam became strong.
D) Because the scholars disagreed on its validity.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that the mu'allaf category has lapsed permanently due to the cessation of its cause, confirmed by the ijma of the Companions, and this is not naskh.

Zakat — Almsgiving
intermediate
8 min
The Mu'allafah Share: Termination by Cessation of Cause
Explains the cessation of a zakat category by ijma' and its rationale.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that the mu'allafah qulubuhum share is no longer given and distinguish between abrogation and termination of a ruling due to cessation of its effective cause.

Core Idea
A ruling ends when its cause ends—no abrogation needed.

This lesson examines the eighth category of zakat recipients, 'those whose hearts are reconciled' (al-mu'allafah qulubuhum). The Quran lists them, but the Companions, led by 'Umar and Abu Bakr, agreed to stop giving them after the Prophet's death. At first glance, this may seem like abrogation of a Quranic ruling, but the Hanafi analysis reveals it is a termination based on the disappearance of the effective cause. Understanding this principle is crucial for proper application of zakat today.

Guided Path
1
Identify the category and its original ruling
Locate the mu'allafah qulubuhum in the Quranic verse (At-Tawbah 9:60) and understand that the Prophet used to give them zakat to reconcile hearts, strengthen faith, or prevent harm.
The original ruling was permissibility (mubah) with the condition that the cause (need for reconciliation) existed.
2
Understand the historical context and the Companions' decision
After the Prophet's death, some individuals approached Abu Bakr and 'Umar for their share. 'Umar argued that Islam was now strong, so the cause no longer existed. Abu Bakr agreed, and the Companions present formed a consensus (ijma') to stop giving.
This was not a personal opinion but an ijtihad based on the disappearance of the 'illa.
3
Distinguish between abrogation (naskh) and termination of cause
The objector asks: How can ijma' abrogate a Quranic ruling? The response: This is not naskh, but rather the natural ending of a ruling when its effective cause ceases. The ruling was attached to the cause; when the cause ends, the ruling ends.
In Hanafi usul, this is known as 'zawal al-hukm bi zawal 'illatihi'.
4
State the consensus and its authority
The ijma' of the Companions on this point is a binding proof. The Hanafi school accepts it as the basis for the current ruling that the mu'allafah share is not operative.
Other schools also accept this ijma', though some (e.g., Shafi'i) consider the category still potentially applicable in certain circumstances.
5
Apply the principle today
A zakat payer today should not allocate funds to someone claiming to be mu'allafah, because the effective cause (weakness of Islam and need for conciliation) no longer exists in a general sense. Zakat must be distributed to the remaining seven categories.
This does not preclude giving voluntary charity to new Muslims to support their faith; it only concerns the obligatory zakat.

Chapter of the Categories of Recipients. (The Chapter of the Categories) [1] His statement: Chapter of the Categories; i.e., the categories of zakat and 'ushr, and they are also categories for sadaqat al-fitr, kaffarah, nadhr, and other obligatory charities. The basis for it is the statement of Allah Most High: 'Indeed the charities are for the poor, the needy, the workers over it, those whose hearts are reconciled, for freeing captives, for debtors, in the way of Allah, and the wayfarer.' So these are eight categories. And one category has fallen from them, namely 'those whose hearts are reconciled.' The Messenger of Allah (peace and blessings be upon him) used to give them so that they would become Muslim or their people would become Muslim due to their Islam. Among them were those who had embraced Islam but had weakness in their hearts, so he would give them to stabilize Islam in their heart. And among them were those whom he would give out of fear of their evil and harm. When the Messenger of Allah passed away, they came to Abu Bakr (may Allah be pleased with him) to take their share, and to 'Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) [they said something]. 'Umar said: 'The Messenger of Allah used to give you so as to reconcile your hearts to Islam. But today Allah has honored His religion. There is nothing between us and you except the sword or Islam.' So they went back to Abu Bakr and said: 'Are you the caliph or 'Umar?' So Abu Bakr affirmed the opinion of 'Umar and abolished their share, and this was in the presence of the Companions, so it became a consensus from them on its cessation. Thus in 'Al-Binayah', etc. ... Then the question: How can the consensus of the Companions and the opinions of men abrogate the share mentioned in the Quran and Sunnah? The answer: There is no abrogation here, rather it is of the type of the termination of something due to the termination of its cause. Its verification is in the books of Usul. (Footnotes with supporting narrations and further explanation).
Source pages p. 469

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof discussing the eighth category of zakat recipients—'those whose hearts are reconciled' (mu'allafah qulubuhum)—and the legal reasoning for its cessation after the Prophet's death. The passage begins by citing the Quranic verse (At-Tawbah 9:60) which lists eight categories. It then states that one category, the mu'allafah, has been dropped. The author explains the historical context: the Prophet used to give such individuals to attract them to Islam, to stabilize their faith, or to prevent harm. After his death, some came to seek their share, but 'Umar ibn al-Khattab argued that the cause had ended because Islam had become strong. Abu Bakr agreed, and the Companions concurred, establishing a consensus (ijma') on the cessation of this share. The passage then addresses the objection that consensus cannot abrogate an explicit Quranic ruling. The response clarifies that this is not abrogation (naskh) but rather the termination of a ruling due to the disappearance of its effective cause ('illa). In Hanafi usul, when a ruling is attached to a condition or cause, it ends when that cause ceases to exist. The author supports this with a detailed analysis in the footnotes, showing that the mu'allafah status is a contingent attribute, and when the need for reconciliation no longer exists, the share is no longer given.

Key Terms
المؤلفة قلوبهم (al-mu'allafah qulubuhum): Those whose hearts are reconciled—individuals given zakat to attract them to Islam or strengthen their faith.
إجماع (ijma'): Consensus of the Companions on the cessation of the mu'allafah share.
سبب (sabab): Effective cause or reason for a ruling. In this context, the cause is the weakness of Islam and the need to reconcile hearts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The mu'allafah share is not given today because its effective cause (weakness of Islam) no longer exists, as established by ijma'.

Definitions of Faqir, Miskin, and Zakat Worker
Defines the first three categories of zakat recipients

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the Hanafi definitions of faqir and miskin, and state the compensation criteria for the zakat worker.

Core Idea
The faqir has some minimal assets; the miskin has none; only the miskin may beg, but both can receive zakat.

This passage defines the first three of the eight zakat recipients. Understanding the precise distinction between faqir (poor) and miskin (needy) is crucial for proper zakat distribution. The Hanafi school uniquely defines these categories based on asset possession, affecting eligibility and the permissibility of begging.

Guided Path
1
List the three categories
Read the passage and identify the three types of zakat recipients explicitly mentioned.
The passage begins 'منهم' referring to the eight categories from the Quran.
2
Define each category
Based on the commentary, extract the precise Hanafi definitions: what does each category possess?
Pay attention to the terms 'أدنى شيء' (minimal thing) and 'لا شيء له' (nothing).
3
Apply the legal consequences
Determine the permissibility of begging and eligibility to receive zakat for each category.
The passage states that the miskin may ask, but the faqir may not, though both can receive zakat.
4
Note the cross-madhhab difference
Recognize that other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) may reverse or equate faqir and miskin, affecting distribution priorities.
This ikhtilaf is mentioned in the key terms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
منهم: الفقير : وهو من له أدنى شيء، والمسكينُ : مَن لا شيء له ، وعامل الصدقة، فيعطى بقدر عمله.  [١] قوله : وهو من له أدنى شيء ؛ أي شيء قليل، وهو دون النصاب، أو قدر نصاب غير نام مستغرق في الحاجة كدارِ السكنى وعبيد الخدمة، وثياب البذلة، وآلات الحرفة، وكتب العلم لمن يحتاج إليها. كذا في «البحر»)، وغيره.  [٢] قوله : من لاشيء له ؛ فيحتاج إلى المسألة لقوته وما يواري بدنه، ويحلّ له ذلك بخلاف الأوّل، فإنّه لا يحل له السؤال، لكن يحلّ صرف الزكاة إليه بعد أن كان فقيراً. كذا في «فتح القدير»  [٣]قوله : وعامل الصدقة ؛ سواء كان ساعياً أو عاشراً، وقد مرَّ الفرق بينهما في باب العاشر فيعطى من الزكاة بقدر ما يكفيه وأعوانه وعياله، مدة ذهابه وإيابه ؛ لأنه فرغ نفسه لهذا العمل .
Among them are: the poor (al-faqir): he who possesses something minimal; the needy (al-miskin): he who possesses nothing; and the worker over charity (amil al-sadaqah): he is given according to his work. [1] His statement: 'he who possesses something minimal' means a little thing, which is below the nisab, or the amount of a nisab that is not productive and is entirely consumed by need, such as a dwelling house, servant slaves, worn-out clothes, tools of trade, and books of knowledge for one who needs them. Thus in 'Al-Bahr', etc. [2] His statement: 'he who possesses nothing' means he needs to ask for his sustenance and what covers his body, and that is permissible for him, unlike the first, for whom asking is not permissible, but it is permissible to give zakat to him after he was poor. Thus in 'Fath al-Qadir'. [3] His statement: 'and the worker over charity' whether he is a collector (sa'i) or a tithe collector ('ashir). The difference between them has already been mentioned in the chapter of the tithe collector. He is given from the zakat an amount sufficient for him, his assistants, and his dependents, for the duration of his going and returning, because he has devoted himself to this work.
Source pages p. 470

Commentary
This passage is a definition-ruling that clarifies the first three categories of zakat recipients: the poor (faqir), the needy (miskin), and the zakat worker. The author defines the poor as one who possesses something minimal—anything below the nisab, or even a non-productive nisab that is entirely consumed by essential needs like housing, clothing, tools, and books. The needy is one who possesses nothing at all, thus requiring begging for sustenance, which is permissible for him. In contrast, the poor is not allowed to beg, though it is permissible to give him zakat. The third category is the zakat worker, which includes both the collector (sa'i) and the tithe collector ('ashir). He is compensated from zakat funds for his work, covering his own needs and those of his assistants and family during his employment. This passage highlights the Hanafi distinction between faqir and miskin: the former has some assets, the latter has none. This distinction affects rulings on eligibility for zakat and permissibility of begging.

Key Terms
الفقير (al-faqir): One who possesses something minimal, below the nisab, or a non-productive nisab consumed by need.
المسكين (al-miskin): One who possesses nothing at all, thus needing to beg.
عامل الصدقة (amil al-sadaqah): The worker over zakat, including collector (sa'i) and tithe collector ('ashir), paid for his work.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the difference between a faqir and a miskin?
A) Faqir has no property, miskin has some.
B) Faqir has some property, miskin has none.
C) They are identical terms.
D) Faqir is a non-Muslim, miskin is Muslim.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the faqir possesses minimal assets, while the miskin has nothing; only the miskin may beg, but both may receive zakat.

Definitions of Faqir, Miskin, and Amil in Zakat
Defines three categories of zakat recipients: faqir, miskin, and amil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define faqir, miskin, and amil according to the Hanafi school, identifying the conditions that distinguish them and their eligibility to receive zakat.

Core Idea
Faqir has less than nisab; miskin has nothing; amil works for collection and is paid from zakat.

The eighth categories of zakat recipients are defined in Surah al-Tawbah (9:60). This lesson unpacks the Hanafi definitions of three key categories—faqir, miskin, and amil—as explained in the commentary 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah. Understanding these distinctions is essential for correctly distributing zakat and avoiding common errors.

Guided Path
1
Define faqir
Identify that the faqir is one who possesses some property (adna shay') but it is below the nisab, or it amounts to a nisab that is non-productive and consumed by essential needs, such as a dwelling house, servant slaves, ordinary clothes, tools of trade, or books of knowledge.
The faqir is prohibited from begging.
2
Define miskin
Identify that the miskin is one who possesses nothing (la shay'a lahu) and thus is permitted to beg for his sustenance and clothing.
The miskin is allowed to ask for help, unlike the faqir.
3
Define amil
Identify that the amil is the zakat collector, either a sa'i (collector of visible wealth) or an 'ashir (tithe collector at borders). He is paid from the zakat fund according to his work.
Payment covers himself, his assistants, and his dependents for the duration of his travel and work.

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah defines three of the eight categories of zakat recipients mentioned in Surah al-Tawbah (9:60). The author explains that the faqir (poor person) is one who possesses some property but less than the nisab, or possesses a nisab that is not productive (like a house or tools) and is consumed by essential needs. The miskin (needy person) is one who owns nothing and is thus permitted to beg for necessities, while the faqir is not allowed to beg. The zakat collector (amil) is paid from the zakat fund for his work, whether he is a sa'i (who collects from visible wealth) or an 'ashir (who collects at the border). He is given sufficient for himself, his helpers, and his family for the duration of his work, including travel. This is because he has devoted his time to this service.

Key Terms
فقير (faqir): One who possesses something (adna shay') below the nisab, or a non-productive nisab consumed by essential needs, and is prohibited from begging.
مسكين (miskin): One who has nothing (la shay'a lahu) and is permitted to beg for necessities.
عامل الصدقة (amil al-sadaqah): The zakat collector, either a sa'i (collector of visible wealth) or an 'ashir (tithe collector at borders), who is paid from zakat for his work.

The Muallafatu Qulubuhum: The Role of Cause (Manat) in Zakat Recipients
Explains the legal cause for muallafah and its cessation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on muallafatu qulubuhum, explain why it ceased based on the absence of its cause (manat), and distinguish this cessation from abrogation.

Core Idea
Giving zakat to reconcile hearts is wajib when needed; when Islam is strong, the cause ends, and the ruling ends—no abrogation.

The category of muallafatu qulubuhum is one of the eight zakat recipients in the Qur'an. However, the Hanafi school emphasizes that the ruling to give them is not a fixed perpetual obligation but is contingent upon a specific cause: the need to strengthen Islam or prevent harm. This lesson explores the legal reasoning behind this contingent ruling, particularly through the example of Caliph Umar's decision to stop payment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the category and its basis in the Qur'an
Find the verse that mentions muallafatu qulubuhum and note that it establishes an entitlement but is attached to a cause.
2
Locate the manat (cause)
Understand that the cause is the need to attract hearts to Islam or repel harm from Muslims.
3
Analyze the causal link
The ruling (giving zakat) is tied to the cause; when the cause is present, the ruling applies; when absent, it ceases.
This is a key principle in Hanafi usul.
4
Apply to Umar's ijtihad
Umar observed that Islam had become strong and honored, so the need to attract hearts via money had disappeared. He therefore stopped payment.
This action was based on ijtihad, not a new naskh.
5
Recognize cessation is not abrogation
The ruling was not permanently lifted by a new law; it simply ceased because its cause no longer existed. The Qur'anic verse remains valid.
This distinguishes Hanafi from Shafi'i positions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

For their hearts, so the meaning of 'and those whose hearts are to be reconciled' is those whose hearts you attract by familiarity and affection. So attracting their hearts is not a ruling established by the law, rather it is a cause (manat) for a ruling that Allah has attached to it. So whenever this cause is realized, the ruling attached to it (giving them from zakat) is realized, and whenever it is absent, what was attached to it falls away. So the description of reconciling the heart is like the description of poverty, working on collecting zakat, and jihad in the way of Allah as a cause for deserving zakat in those categories, not their mere identities. Allah, in that it was, so Umar's ijtihad was attached to realizing the cause, for he saw that Islam had reached the peak of strength and might in all its aspects until it became a pride for whoever is affiliated with it. So his honor through Islam that overshadowed the earth makes attracting his heart stronger than the money that would be given to him. So there was no longer a need to give them money from zakat. Indeed, because the absence of the ruling is due to the absence of the meaning that calls for it, not by abrogation. So the legality of giving the muallafatu qulubuhum a share of zakat ended with the end of its cause, which was the weakness of the Muslims and the achievement of honor of the religion through it. Reconciling them to Islam by giving money and repelling their harm from Muslims was an honor for the religion in that time, but when the matter of Islam became strong, giving to them became a degradation for the religion, not an honor. So it ended with the end of its cause. And its completion is in Dhabit al-Maslaha (p. 143-144) and Kashf al-Asrar (3:167) and others.
Source pages p. 470

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah discusses the category of muallafatu qulubuhum (those whose hearts are to be reconciled to Islam). The author explains that the ruling to give them zakat is not a fixed commandment but is contingent upon a cause (manat): the need to strengthen Islam or repel harm. This cause is like poverty for the poor or work for the collector. The example of Caliph Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) is cited, who stopped giving this category because Islam had become strong. The author emphasizes that this cessation was not due to abrogation (naskh) but due to the disappearance of the cause. This is a key point in Hanafi usul al-fiqh: rulings tied to causes (manat) cease when the cause ceases. The author cites sources like Dhabit al-Maslaha and Kashf al-Asrar.

Key Terms
مؤلفة قلوبهم (muallafatu qulubuhum): Those whose hearts are to be reconciled to Islam, given zakat to attract them to the faith or prevent harm.
مناط (manat): The legal cause or effective reason upon which a ruling is contingent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Because the cause (weakness of Islam) no longer existed
C) Because the recipients became Muslims
D) Because the amount of zakat was insufficient

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, rulings attached to a cause (manat) cease when the cause is absent, and this is not abrogation but correct application.

Four Additional Zakat Recipients: Mukatab, Madiun, Fi Sabilillah, and Ibn al-Sabil
Defines four additional zakat categories with an intra-Hanafi dispute on fi sabilillah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four additional categories of zakat recipients, identify the conditions for each, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding the interpretation of 'fi sabilillah'.

Core Idea
Fi sabilillah is not only for jihad—Muhammad ibn al-Hasan includes stranded pilgrims, broadening the category.

The Quran (9:60) lists eight categories of zakat recipients. Beyond the well-known poor and needy, four more categories are often misunderstood: the mukatab (slave seeking freedom), the madiun (debtor), those 'in the way of Allah' (fi sabilillah), and the stranded traveler (ibn al-sabil). This lesson clarifies each category's definition and highlights an important intra-Hanafi disagreement on the scope of 'fi sabilillah' that affects contemporary zakat distribution.

Guided Path
1
Identify each category
Name the four categories: mukatab, madiun, fi sabilillah, ibn al-sabil. Understand what each term means literally and technically.
Mukatab is a slave with a manumission contract; madiun is a debtor; fi sabilillah is 'in the way of Allah'; ibn al-sabil is a traveler.
2
Examine conditions for eligibility
For each category, note the condition that triggers zakat eligibility. For the madiun, he must not own a nisab surplus after debts; for ibn al-sabil, he must have wealth at home but no access to it; for the mukatab, he is aided to free himself; for fi sabilillah, the interpretation differs.
The passage explicitly mentions the condition for the debtor and the traveler.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on fi sabilillah
Abu Yusuf restricts fi sabilillah to fighters cut off from supplies. Muhammad extends it to pilgrims cut off from provisions. Understand the basis: each sees the phrase 'in the way of Allah' differently—narrowly as jihad or broadly as all righteous deeds.
The text in al-Bada'i suggests Muhammad's view includes all acts of obedience.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical effect: under Abu Yusuf, only stranded jihad fighters receive zakat; under Muhammad, stranded pilgrims and others on legitimate journeys also qualify. This difference affects who can be given zakat in real-world scenarios.
The mufta bihi is not settled in this text; both views remain viable.

Source pages p. 471

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah defines four more categories of zakat recipients. The mukatab (slave under contract of manumission) is given zakat to help free himself. A debtor (madiun) who has no surplus nisab after paying debts is eligible. The category 'fi sabilillah' (in the way of Allah) has an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Yusuf interprets it as a fighter cut off from resources, while Muhammad interprets it as a pilgrim cut off from provisions. The author notes that the commentator of Ghayat al-Bayan preferred Abu Yusuf's view, but al-Bada'i mentions Muhammad's view includes all good deeds. Also, anyone who devotes himself to Muslim public service, like a judge, may take a salary from zakat even if rich, just as a rich traveler (ibn sabil) may take zakat when in need.

Key Terms
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has made a contract (kitaba) with his master to earn his freedom by paying a sum, and is given zakat to help fulfill the contract.
مديون (madiun): A debtor who does not own a nisab surplus after deducting debts, eligible for zakat.
في سبيل الله (fi sabilillah): In the way of Allah: interpreted by Abu Yusuf as a stranded fighter, by Muhammad as a stranded pilgrim, and by some as covering all righteous works.
ابن السبيل (ibn al-sabil): A traveler who has wealth at home but is stranded without resources; eligible for zakat even if rich at home.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) He must be in debt due to a necessary expense.
D) He must be unable to work.

Key Takeaway
The eight zakat recipients include the mukatab, madiun, fi sabilillah (with an intra-Hanafi dispute), and ibn al-sabil, each with specific conditions.

Definitions of Mukatab, Madyun, Fi Sabil Allah, and Ibn al-Sabil
Defines four zakat recipients with a focus on the dispute over 'fi sabil Allah'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definitions of the four remaining zakat recipients according to the Hanafi school, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding 'fi sabil Allah'.

Core Idea
Fi sabil Allah: Abu Yusuf says stranded fighter; Muhammad says stranded pilgrim — both valid within Hanafi.

This passage from the commentary on Al-Hidayah completes the list of zakat recipients by defining the last four categories: the mukatab (slave under contract), the madyun (debtor), fi sabil Allah (in the way of Allah), and ibn al-sabil (wayfarer). It also reveals a subtle intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani on the scope of 'fi sabil Allah'. Understanding these definitions is essential for correctly disbursing zakat.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four categories
Locate the definitions of mukatab, madyun, fi sabil Allah, and ibn al-sabil in the Arabic. Note that the passage first lists them, then elaborates.
The categories are given in the same order as Quran 9:60.
2
Analyze the definition of fi sabil Allah
Compare the two interpretations: Abu Yusuf defines it as the stranded fighter (munqati' al-ghuzat); Muhammad defines it as the stranded pilgrim (munqati' al-hajjaj). Recognize that this is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
The commentator notes that Muhammad's view is broader, including all acts of obedience.
3
Understand the ruling for the rich in public service
Note the additional point: someone devoted to Muslim affairs (like a judge) can receive zakat even if rich, by analogy to ibn al-sabil.
This is a separate application of need-based eligibility.
4
Trace the sources cited
The passage refers to Al-Hidayah and Al-Bada'i. Recognize that this is a later commentary (Umda al-Ri'aya) explaining earlier matn.
This helps situate the text in the Hanafi tradition.

Source pages p. 471

Commentary
This passage defines the remaining four categories of zakat recipients after the first four (fuqara, masakin, amil, mu'allafatu qulubuhum, and riqab). The rulings are: Al-Mukatab: a slave under a contract of manumission; he is given zakat to help free himself. Al-Madyun: a debtor who does not possess a nisab (wealth surplus over his debt); the debt is considered a need, so he may receive zakat even if he has some assets. Fi Sabil Allah: this is disputed between the two leading Hanafi imams. Abu Yusuf interprets it as a stranded fighter (munqati' al-ghuzat) who cannot reach the Muslim army due to lack of means, even if he has wealth at home. Muhammad interprets it as a stranded pilgrim (munqati' al-hajjaj). The commentator notes that Muhammad's view is broader, encompassing all acts of obedience. Ibn al-Sabil: a wayfarer who has wealth at home but not with him during travel; he is given zakat to meet his immediate need. The passage also states that a rich person engaged in public service (like a judge) can receive zakat if in need, analogously to the wayfarer. The author cites al-Hidayah and al-Bada'i as sources.

Key Terms
المكاتب (al-mukatab): A slave who has a written contract with his master to earn and pay for his freedom; he is given zakat to help fulfill the contract.
مديون (madyun): A person in debt who does not possess a nisab (minimum wealth) beyond the debt amount; his debt makes him eligible for zakat.
في سبيل الله (fi sabil Allah): Literally 'in the way of Allah'; in this context, it refers to a person engaged in a good deed who is stranded and in need, specifically a fighter (Abu Yusuf) or a pilgrim (Muhammad).
ابن السبيل (ibn al-sabil): A traveler who has wealth at home but is without means during his journey; he is given zakat to meet his immediate need.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following correctly describes the dispute about 'fi sabil Allah'?
A) Abu Yusuf says it is the stranded pilgrim; Muhammad says it is the stranded fighter.
B) Abu Yusuf says it is the stranded fighter; Muhammad says it is the stranded pilgrim.
C) Both imams agree it means any stranded person in need.
D) Abu Hanifa interprets it as a public servant like a judge.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school defines eight zakat recipients; the category 'fi sabil Allah' has a valid intra-school dispute between a narrow (fighter) and broad (all good deeds) interpretation.

Distributing Zakat to One Category: The Hanafi Position
Ruling that zakat giver may allocate to some categories, countering Shafi'i view, with linguistic analysis of the lam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school permits distributing zakat to a single category, and contrast this with the Shafi'i requirement using the linguistic analysis of the lam.

Core Idea
The lam in zakat verse indicates genus, not comprehensiveness — so giving to one category fulfills the obligation.

This passage addresses a key difference between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools: must zakat be distributed to all eight categories? The Hanafi school says no, the giver may give to some or even one category. The ruling hinges on the linguistic function of the lam (ل) in Quran 9:60. The commentator explains the Hanafi usul principle and shows that the difference with Shafi'i is largely verbal, with practical impact in wills and endowments.

Guided Path
1
State the practical question
Identify the central fiqh issue: Must a zakat giver distribute zakat to all eight categories (asnaf) of recipients, or can he select only some?
The matn states the Hanafi answer upfront: he may give to all or to some.
2
Identify the opposing Shafi'i position and its basis
Imam al-Shafi'i requires distribution to all eight categories, with at least three recipients per category. His basis: the lam in 'li al-fuqara' indicates comprehensiveness (istighraq), and the plural minimum is three.
The text references the Shafi'i view explicitly.
3
Analyze the Hanafi linguistic argument about al-lam
The Hanafi response uses usul al-fiqh: the lam can indicate external covenant ('ahd), comprehensiveness (istighraq), or genus (jins). Since there is no prior covenant, and comprehensiveness is not required, the lam indicates genus. Thus, the plural denotes the category as a type, not each individual.
The commentator cites al-Nahr and states the difference is verbal.
4
Recognize the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
The difference appears in non-zakat contexts, such as wills and endowments. For example, if a testator says 'give in the way of Allah', Hanafis interpret broadly, Shafi'is restrict to jihad.
The passage mentions this explicitly.
5
Evaluate the tarjih and mufta bihi
The Hanafi position (giving to one category) is relied upon for fatwa. The disagreement is legitimate and verbal, but the practical ruling in zakat is clear.
The text implies the Hanafi view is the operative one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وللمزكي صرفها إلى كلّهم أو إلى بعضهم: احتراز عن قول الشافعي، إذ عنده لا بد أن يصرف إلى جميع الأصناف، فيُعطي من كل صنف ثلاثة؛ لأنّ أقل الجمع ثلاثة. ونحن نقول: إذا دخل اللام على الجمع قال في «النهر»: «الخلف لفظي للاتفاق على أن كل الأصناف سوى العامل يعطون بشرط الفقر، فمنقطع الحج يعطى اتفاقاً». انتهى. نعم فائدة الخلاف تظهر في الوصية والأوقاف ونحوها، فيما إذا قال الموصي: في سبيل الله جلالة. توضيحه: أن أصل اللام أن تكون للعهد الخارجي؛ فإن لم يكن فالاستغراق، فإن لم يكن فالجنس، سواءً كانت داخلة على المفرد أو الجمع، وإذا حملت اللام على الجنس في الجمع يبطل معنى الجمعية، ويراد به نفس الجنس، وقد حقق ذلك في كتب الأصول بما لا مزيد عليه.
And the zakat-giver may distribute it to all of them or to some of them: this is a safeguard against the statement of al-Shafi'i, for according to him he must distribute to all categories, giving three from each category because the minimum plural is three. And we say: when the lam enters on a plural, it was said in al-Nahr: 'The difference is verbal, since there is agreement that all categories except the worker are given conditioned on poverty, so the stranded pilgrim is given by agreement.' End. Yes, the benefit of the difference appears in bequests and endowments and the like, when the testator says: 'in the way of Allah' collectively. Its clarification: the origin of the lam is for external covenant; if not, then for istighraq (comprehensiveness); if not, then for genus, whether it enters on a singular or plural. And when the lam is carried on the genus in the plural, the meaning of plurality becomes invalid, and the genus itself is intended. This has been verified in the books of usul beyond which there is nothing more.
Source pages p. 472

Commentary
This passage addresses a key difference between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding the distribution of zakat. The matn states that the zakat giver may give to all eight categories or to only some of them. This is a direct refutation of Imam al-Shafi'i's requirement to give to all categories, and to give at least three recipients per category (because the minimum plural is three). The Hanafi response is based on a linguistic principle concerning the lam (ل) of specification in the Quranic verse (9:60). According to Hanafi usul, when the lam enters a plural noun like 'li al-fuqara', it can indicate the genus (jins) rather than each individual. Therefore, giving to one category fulfills the obligation. The commentator clarifies that the origin of the lam is for a previously mentioned specific group (al-'ahd al-khariji); if that is not present, it indicates absolute comprehensiveness (al-istighraq); if not, it indicates the genus. Since the categories in the verse are not previously specified, the lam is for genus, so the plural does not require covering all individuals. The difference is largely verbal because the Shafi'i condition of poverty for all recipients except the worker is agreed upon. The practical effect appears in wills and endowments where the phrase 'fi sabil Allah' is used.

Key Terms
اللام (al-lam): The preposition 'li' (ل) used in Quran 9:60 for each category of zakat recipients; its linguistic function determines if distribution to all is required.
الاستغراق (al-istighraq): Linguistic term meaning that the lam indicates inclusion of every individual of the noun; in this dispute, Shafi'i argues the lam is istighraq.
الجنس (al-jins): Genus; when the lam indicates the genus, it denotes the category as a whole, not individuals. Hanafis argue this allows giving to one category.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, why is it permissible to give zakat to only one category of recipients?
A) Because the Prophet (PBUH) gave all zakat to one category.
B) Because the lam in 'li al-fuqara' indicates the genus, not comprehensive allocation.
C) Because the Quranic verse gives an option to choose any category.
D) Because poor people have the strongest claim.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits giving zakat to one or some categories because the lam in the Quranic verse indicates genus, not comprehensiveness.

Distribution of Zakat: Hanafi Permissibility vs Shafi'i Requirement
Dispute on whether Zakat must be distributed to all eight categories

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on Zakat distribution, explain the linguistic argument based on the 'lam' of genus, and contrast it with the Shafi'i requirement.

Core Idea
Zakat may be given to one category because the Quranic 'lam' indicates genus, not generality.

This passage addresses a key dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools: must Zakat be distributed to all eight categories of recipients, or is it permissible to give it to just one? The Hanafi school holds the latter view, based on a linguistic analysis of the definite article in the Quranic verse. Let us unpack the argument and its practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the point of dispute
The Shafi'i school requires distribution to at least three individuals from each of the eight categories, while the Hanafi school allows giving to one category only.
2
Examine the Hanafi linguistic argument
The definite article 'lam' in 'lil-fuqara' is interpreted as indicating genus (jins) rather than generality. Why must it be genus?
3
Trace the Hanafi proof
The argument relies on the principle that 'lam' is for external specification first, then generality, then genus. Since specification is impossible and generality leads to hardship, it defaults to genus.
4
Note the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
A Hanafi may give all Zakat to one poor person; a Shafi'i must give to at least 24 recipients (three from each of eight categories).
